class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilojoule_per_mole" title="Kilojoule per mole">kJ/mol</a> </td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_vaporization" title="Enthalpy .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_hydrogen" title="Template:Infobox hydrogen"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_hydrogen" title="Template talk:Infobox hydrogen"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_hydrogen&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hydrogen</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbol</a> <b>H</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 1. Hydrogen is the lightest element. At <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a> hydrogen is a <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic molecules</a> having the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">formula</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>. It is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">colorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_smell" title="Sense of smell">odorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">tasteless</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> non-toxic, and highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combustible" title="Combustible">combustible</a>. Hydrogen is the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">most abundant</a> chemical substance in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, constituting roughly 75% of all <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">normal</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[note 1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">Stars</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> are mainly composed of hydrogen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_state" title="Plasma state">plasma state</a>. Most of the hydrogen on Earth exists in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_geometry" title="Molecular geometry">molecular forms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. For the most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of hydrogen (symbol <sup>1</sup>H) each <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> has one <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, and no <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>.
</p><p>In the early <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, the formation of protons, the nuclei of hydrogen, occurred during the first second after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. The emergence of neutral hydrogen atoms throughout the universe occurred about 370,000 years later during the <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination epoch</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> had cooled enough for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a> to remain bound to protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonmetallic" title="Nonmetallic">nonmetallic</a>, except at extremely high pressures, and readily forms a single <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bond</a> with most nonmetallic elements, forming compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and nearly all <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. Hydrogen plays a particularly important role in <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">acid–base reactions</a> because these reactions usually involve the exchange of protons between soluble molecules. In <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic compounds</a>, hydrogen can take the form of a negative charge (i.e., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a>) where it is known as a <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a>, or as a positively charged (i.e., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cation</a>) <a href="/wiki/Chemical_species" title="Chemical species">species</a> denoted by the symbol <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>. The <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span> cation is simply a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> (symbol <b>p</b>) but its behavior in <a href="/wiki/Aqueous_solution" title="Aqueous solution">aqueous solutions</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic compounds</a> involves <a href="/wiki/Electric-field_screening" title="Electric-field screening">screening</a> of its <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> by nearby <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> molecules or anions. Because hydrogen is the only neutral atom for which the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> can be solved analytically,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laursen04_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laursen04-12">[11]</a></sup> the study of its energetics and chemical bonding has played a key role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>.
</p><p>Hydrogen gas was first artificially produced in the early 16th century by the reaction of acids on metals. In 1766–1781, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> was the first to recognize that hydrogen gas was a discrete substance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> and that it produces water when burned, the property for which it was later named: in Greek, hydrogen means "water-former".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Industrial production</a> is mainly from <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, oil reforming, or <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[13]</a></sup> A small percentage is also produced using more energy-intensive methods such as the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Most hydrogen is used near the site of its production, the two largest uses being <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> processing (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> production, mostly for the fertilizer market. It can be burned to produce heat or combined with oxygen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">fuel cells</a> to generate electricity directly, with water being the only emissions at the point of usage. Hydrogen atoms (but not gaseous molecules) are problematic in <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> because they can <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittle</a> many metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers_1999_1057–1064_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers_1999_1057–1064-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Production"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Production</span></a>

codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="567880" data-framerate="23.976" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/09/19._%D0%95%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%BF%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%B0_%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B4%D1%83%D1%85.webm/19._%D0%95%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%BF%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%B0_%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B4%D1%83%D1%85.webm.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19._%D0%95%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%BF%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%B0_%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B4%D1%83%D1%85.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Combustion of hydrogen with the oxygen in the air. When the bottom cap is removed, allowing air to enter at the bottom, the hydrogen in the container rises out of top and burns as it mixes with the air.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg"><img alt="A black cup-like object hanging by its bottom with blue glow coming out of its opening." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1612" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/170px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/255px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/340px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engine</a> burnt hydrogen with oxygen, producing a nearly invisible flame at full thrust.</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen gas (<b>dihydrogen</b> or molecular hydrogen)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> is highly flammable:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>(g) + O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>(g) → 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O(l)</span> (572 kJ/2 mol = 286 kJ/mol = 141.865 MJ/kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[note 2]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_combustion" title="Enthalpy of combustion">enthalpy of combustion</a> is −286 kJ/mol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen gas forms explosive mixtures with air in concentrations from 4–74%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> and with chlorine at 5–95%. The explosive reactions may be triggered by spark, heat, or sunlight. The hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Autoignition_temperature" title="Autoignition temperature">autoignition temperature</a>, the temperature of spontaneous ignition in air, is 500 °C (932 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flame">Flame</span></h4>
<p>Pure <a href="/wiki/Oxyhydrogen" title="Oxyhydrogen">hydrogen-oxygen</a> flames emit <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light and with high oxygen mix are nearly invisible to the naked eye, as illustrated by the faint plume of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engine</a>, compared to the highly visible plume of a <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster</a>, which uses an <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate_composite_propellant" title="Ammonium perchlorate composite propellant">ammonium perchlorate composite</a>. The detection of a burning hydrogen leak may require a <a href="/wiki/Flame_detector" title="Flame detector">flame detector</a>; such leaks can be very dangerous. Hydrogen flames in other conditions are blue, resembling blue natural gas flames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_disaster" title="Hindenburg disaster">destruction of the Hindenburg airship</a> was a notorious example of hydrogen combustion and the cause is still debated. The visible flames in the photographs were the result of carbon compounds in the airship skin burning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactants">Reactants</span></h4>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is unreactive compared to diatomic elements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halogens" title="Halogens">halogens</a> or oxygen. The thermodynamic basis of this low reactivity is the very strong H–H bond, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bond_dissociation_energy" title="Bond dissociation energy">bond dissociation energy</a> of 435.7 kJ/mol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> The kinetic basis of the low reactivity is the nonpolar nature of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> and its weak polarizability. It spontaneously reacts with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> The reactivity of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is strongly affected by the presence of metal catalysts. Thus, while mixtures of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> with <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> or air combust readily when heated to at least 500 °C by a spark or flame, they do not react at room temperature in the absence of a catalyst.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_energy_levels">Electron energy levels</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">Hydrogen atom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_atom.svg"><img alt="Drawing of a light-gray large sphere with a cut off quarter and a black small sphere and numbers 1.7x10−5 illustrating their relative diameters." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="270" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/220px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/330px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/440px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_atom.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a hydrogen atom with size of central proton shown, and the atomic diameter shown as about twice the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> radius (image not to scale)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy level</a> of the electron in a hydrogen atom is −13.6 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> which is equivalent to an ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> of roughly 91 <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">nm</a> wavelength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The energy levels of hydrogen can be calculated fairly accurately using the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> of the atom, which conceptualizes the electron as "orbiting" the proton in analogy to the Earth's orbit of the Sun. However, the atomic electron and proton are held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>, while planets and celestial objects are held by <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Because of the discretization of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> postulated in early <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> by Bohr, the electron in the Bohr model can only occupy certain allowed distances from the proton, and therefore only certain allowed energies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>A more accurate description of the hydrogen atom comes from a purely quantum mechanical treatment that uses the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Feynman</a> <a href="/wiki/Path_integral_formulation" title="Path integral formulation">path integral formulation</a> to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probability density</a> of the electron around the proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> The most complicated treatments allow for the small effects of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_polarization" title="Vacuum polarization">vacuum polarization</a>. In the quantum mechanical treatment, the electron in a ground state hydrogen atom has no angular momentum at all—illustrating how the "planetary orbit" differs from electron motion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spin_isomers">Spin isomers</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">Spin isomers of hydrogen</a></div>
<p>Molecular <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> exists as two <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">spin isomers</a>, i.e. compounds that differ only in the <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin states</a> of their nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uigi_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-uigi-31">[29]</a></sup> In the <b>orthohydrogen</b> form, the spins of the two nuclei are parallel, forming a spin <a href="/wiki/Triplet_state" title="Triplet state">triplet state</a> having a <a href="/wiki/Spin_quantum_number#Total_spin_of_an_atom_or_molecule" title="Spin quantum number">total molecular spin</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S=1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5e2b58c1aaaf2718fb801e97bf21d1f72726372" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.76ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>; in the <b>parahydrogen</b> form the spins are antiparallel and form a spin <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet state</a> having spin <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ae0fc3192b864f55a46749d8a64e7cf7783d04c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.76ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. The equilibrium ratio of ortho- to para-hydrogen depends on temperature. At room temperature or warmer, equilibrium hydrogen gas contains about 25% of the para form and 75% of the ortho form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2012_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green2012-32">[30]</a></sup> The ortho form is an <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a>, having higher energy than the para form by 1.455 kJ/mol,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanckInstitut_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanckInstitut-33">[31]</a></sup> and it converts to the para form over the course of several minutes when cooled to low temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> The thermal properties of the forms differ because they differ in their allowed <a href="/wiki/Rotational%E2%80%93vibrational_spectroscopy" title="Rotational–vibrational spectroscopy">rotational quantum states</a>, resulting in different thermal properties such as the heat capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ortho-to-para ratio in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is an important consideration in the <a href="/wiki/Liquefaction" title="Liquefaction">liquefaction</a> and storage of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>: the conversion from ortho to para is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> and produces enough heat to evaporate a most of the liquid if not converted first to parahydrogen during the cooling process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amos98_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amos98-36">[34]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">Catalysts</a> for the ortho-para interconversion, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferric_oxide" title="Ferric oxide">ferric oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a> compounds, are used during hydrogen cooling to avoid this loss of liquid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Svadlenak_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Svadlenak-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases">Phases</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="2372" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg/220px-Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg/330px-Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg/440px-Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_ampoule.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrogen gas is colorless and transparent, here contained in a glass <a href="/wiki/Ampoule" title="Ampoule">ampoule</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_hydrogen.png"><img alt="Phase diagram of hydrogen on logarithmic scales. Lines show boundaries between phases, with the end of the liquid-gas line indicating the critical point. The triple point of hydrogen is just off-scale to the left." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Phase_diagram_of_hydrogen.png/350px-Phase_diagram_of_hydrogen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Phase_diagram_of_hydrogen.png 1.5x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_hydrogen.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">Phase diagram</a> of hydrogen. The temperature and pressure scales are <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a>, so one unit corresponds to a 10× change. The left edge corresponds to 10<sup>5</sup> Pa, which is about <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">Gaseous</a> hydrogen</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">Liquid hydrogen</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:Hydrogen_compounds" title="Category:Hydrogen compounds">Category:Hydrogen compounds</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Covalent_and_organic_compounds">Covalent and organic compounds</span></h4>
<p>While <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is not very reactive under standard conditions, it does form compounds with most elements. Hydrogen can form compounds with elements that are more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronegative" title="Electronegative">electronegative</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> (F, Cl, Br, I), or <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>; in these compounds hydrogen takes on a partial positive charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> When bonded to a more electronegative element, particularly <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, hydrogen can participate in a form of medium-strength noncovalent bonding with another electronegative element with a lone pair, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonding</a> that is critical to the stability of many biological molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Hydrogen also forms compounds with less electronegative elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a>, where it takes on a partial negative charge. These compounds are often known as <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen forms a vast array of compounds with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, and an even vaster array with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heteroatoms" title="Heteroatoms">heteroatoms</a> that, because of their general association with living things, are called <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydrocarbon_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-hydrocarbon-42">[40]</a></sup> The study of their properties is known as <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> and their study in the context of living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> is known as <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> By some definitions, "organic" compounds are only required to contain carbon. However, most of them also contain hydrogen, and because it is the carbon-hydrogen bond that gives this class of compounds most of its particular chemical characteristics, carbon-hydrogen bonds are required in some definitions of the word "organic" in chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydrocarbon_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-hydrocarbon-42">[40]</a></sup> Millions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> are known, and they are usually formed by complicated pathways that seldom involve elemental hydrogen.
</p><p>Hydrogen is highly soluble in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeshita_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takeshita-45">[43]</a></sup> and is soluble in both nanocrystalline and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_metal" title="Amorphous metal">amorphous metals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchheim1_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim1-46">[44]</a></sup> Hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> in metals is influenced by local distortions or impurities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" title="Crystal lattice">crystal lattice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchheim2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim2-47">[45]</a></sup> These properties may be useful when hydrogen is purified by passage through hot <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> disks, but the gas's high solubility is a metallurgical problem, contributing to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittlement</a> of many metals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers_1999_1057–1064_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers_1999_1057–1064-17">[16]</a></sup> complicating the design of pipelines and storage tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides">Hydrides</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">Hydride</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NaH.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="190" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/NaH.jpg/220px-NaH.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/NaH.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NaH.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydride" title="Sodium hydride">sodium hydride</a></div></div></div>
<p>Compounds of hydrogen are often called <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a>, a term that is used fairly loosely. The term "hydride" suggests that the H atom has acquired a negative or anionic character, denoted <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">−</sup></span>, and is used when hydrogen forms a compound with a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electropositive" title="Electropositive">electropositive</a> element. The existence of the <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride anion</a>, suggested by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> in 1916 for group 1 and 2 salt-like hydrides, was demonstrated by Moers in 1920 by the electrolysis of molten <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">lithium hydride</a> (LiH), producing a <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometric</a> quantity of hydrogen at the anode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moers_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moers-49">[47]</a></sup> For hydrides other than group 1 and 2 metals, the term is quite misleading, considering the low electronegativity of hydrogen. An exception in group 2 hydrides is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">BeH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>, which is polymeric. In <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a>, the <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">[AlH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub>]<sup class="template-chem2-sup">−</sup></span> anion carries hydridic centers firmly attached to the Al(III).
</p><p>Although hydrides can be formed with almost all main-group elements, the number and combination of possible compounds varies widely; for example, more than 100 binary borane hydrides are known, but only one binary aluminium hydride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downs_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Downs-50">[48]</a></sup> Binary <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> hydride has not yet been identified, although larger complexes exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbs_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbs-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">inorganic chemistry</a>, hydrides can also serve as <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligands</a> that link two metal centers in a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a>. This function is particularly common in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_13_element" title="Group 13 element">group 13 elements</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">boranes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> hydrides) and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> complexes, as well as in clustered <a href="/wiki/Carborane" title="Carborane">carboranes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protons_and_acids">Protons and acids</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">Acid–base reaction</a></div>
<p>Oxidation of hydrogen removes its electron and gives <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_ion" title="Hydrogen ion"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span></a>, which contains no electrons and a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> which is usually composed of one proton. That is why <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span> is often called a proton. This species is central to discussion of <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory</a>, acids are proton donors, while bases are proton acceptors.
</p><p>A bare proton, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>, cannot exist in solution or in ionic crystals because of its unstoppable attraction to other atoms or molecules with electrons. Except at the high temperatures associated with plasmas, such protons cannot be removed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron clouds</a> of atoms and molecules, and will remain attached to them. However, the term 'proton' is sometimes used loosely and metaphorically to refer to positively charged or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cationic</a> hydrogen attached to other species in this fashion, and as such is denoted "<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>" without any implication that any single protons exist freely as a species.
</p><p>To avoid the implication of the naked "solvated proton" in solution, acidic aqueous solutions are sometimes considered to contain a less unlikely fictitious species, termed the "<a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion" (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">[H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O]<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>). However, even in this case, such solvated hydrogen cations are more realistically conceived as being organized into clusters that form species closer to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">[H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">9</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub>]<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Okumura_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Okumura-53">[51]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Oxonium_ion" title="Oxonium ion">oxonium ions</a> are found when water is in acidic solution with other solvents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perdoncin_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perdoncin-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although exotic on Earth, one of the most common ions in the universe is the <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>+</span><span>3</span></span></span> ion, known as <a href="/wiki/Trihydrogen_cation" title="Trihydrogen cation">protonated molecular hydrogen</a> or the trihydrogen cation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carrington_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carrington-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">Isotopes of hydrogen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png"><img alt="Blausen 0530 HydrogenIsotopes.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png/440px-Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png/660px-Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png/880px-Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0530_HydrogenIsotopes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrogen discharge (spectrum) tube</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deuterium discharge (spectrum) tube</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen has three naturally occurring isotopes, denoted <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>. Other, highly unstable nuclei (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>) have been synthesized in the laboratory but not observed in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gurov_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gurov-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korsheninnikov_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korsheninnikov-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span></b> is the most common hydrogen isotope, with an abundance of more than 99.98%. Because the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of this isotope consists of only a single proton, it is given the descriptive but rarely used formal name <i><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">protium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> It is unique among all stable isotopes in having no neutrons; see <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_helium#Helium-2_(diproton)" title="Isotopes of helium">diproton</a> for a discussion of why others do not exist.</li>
<li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span></b>, the other stable hydrogen isotope, is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a></i> and contains one proton and one <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> in the nucleus. All deuterium in the universe is thought to have been produced at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, and has endured since that time. Deuterium is not radioactive, and does not represent a significant toxicity hazard. Water enriched in molecules that include deuterium instead of normal hydrogen is called <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>. Deuterium and its compounds are used as a non-radioactive label in chemical experiments and in solvents for <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR_spectroscopy" title="NMR spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> Heavy water is used as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> and coolant for nuclear reactors. Deuterium is also a potential fuel for commercial <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span></b> is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a></i> and contains one proton and two neutrons in its nucleus. It is radioactive, decaying into <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> through <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 12.32 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-52">[50]</a></sup> It is so radioactive that it can be used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioluminescent_paint" title="Radioluminescent paint">luminous paint</a>, making it useful in such things as watches. The glass prevents the small amount of radiation from getting out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traub95_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Traub95-61">[59]</a></sup> Small amounts of tritium are produced naturally by the interaction of cosmic rays with atmospheric gases; tritium has also been released during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear weapons tests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> It is used in nuclear fusion reactions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> as a tracer in <a href="/wiki/Isotope_geochemistry" title="Isotope geochemistry">isotope geochemistry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> and in specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-powered_lighting" title="Self-powered lighting">self-powered lighting</a> devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> Tritium has also been used in chemical and biological labeling experiments as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiolabel" title="Radiolabel">radiolabel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holte_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-holte-66">[64]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Unique among the elements, distinct names are assigned to its isotopes in common use today. During the early study of radioactivity, various heavy radioactive isotopes were given their own names, but such names are no longer used, except for deuterium and tritium. The symbols D and T (instead of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>) are sometimes used for deuterium and tritium, but the symbol P is already in use for <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and thus is not available for protium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> In its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature" title="IUPAC nomenclature">nomenclatural</a> guidelines, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) allows any of D, T, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> to be used, although <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> are preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Exotic_atom" title="Exotic atom">exotic atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Muonium" title="Muonium">muonium</a> (symbol Mu), composed of an <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">antimuon</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, can also be considered a light radioisotope of hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-69">[67]</a></sup> Because muons decay with lifetime <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000220000000000000♠"></span>2.2 <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">µs</a></span>, muonium is too unstable to exhibit observable chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-70">[68]</a></sup> Nevertheless, muonium compounds are important test cases for <a href="/wiki/Quantum_simulator" title="Quantum simulator">quantum simulation</a>, due to the mass difference between the antimuon and the proton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> and IUPAC nomenclature incorporates such hypothetical compounds as muonium chloride (MuCl) and sodium muonide (NaMu), analogous to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydride" title="Sodium hydride">sodium hydride</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iupac_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-iupac-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_use">Discovery and use</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_hydrogen_technologies" title="Timeline of hydrogen technologies">Timeline of hydrogen technologies</a></div>
<p>In 1671, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> discovered and described the reaction between <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> filings and dilute <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, which results in the production of hydrogen gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> In 1766, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> was the first to recognize hydrogen gas as a discrete substance, by naming the gas from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal-acid_reaction" title="Metal-acid reaction">metal-acid reaction</a> "inflammable air". He speculated that "inflammable air" was in fact identical to the hypothetical substance called "<a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cav766_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-cav766-76">[74]</a></sup> and further finding in 1781 that the gas produces water when burned. He is usually given credit for the discovery of hydrogen as an element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nostrand_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nostrand-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> In 1783, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> gave the element the name hydrogen (from the Greek ὑδρο- <i>hydro</i> meaning "water" and -γενής <i>genes</i> meaning "former")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> when he and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Laplace</a> reproduced Cavendish's finding that water is produced when hydrogen is burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_(by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre)RENEW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2158" data-file-width="1584" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/180px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/270px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/360px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_(by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre)RENEW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antoine-Laurent de Lavoisier</div></div></div>
<p>Lavoisier produced hydrogen for his experiments on mass conservation by reacting a flux of steam with metallic <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> through an incandescent iron tube heated in a fire. Anaerobic oxidation of iron by the protons of water at high temperature can be schematically represented by the set of following reactions:
</p>
<dl><dd>1) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O  →  FeO + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>2) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe + 3 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O  →  Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub> + 3 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>3) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe + 4 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O  →  Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + 4 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Many metals such as <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> undergo a similar reaction with water leading to the production of hydrogen.
</p><p>Hydrogen was <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquefied</a> for the first time by <a href="/wiki/James_Dewar" title="James Dewar">James Dewar</a> in 1898 by using <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_cooling" title="Regenerative cooling">regenerative cooling</a> and his invention, the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_flask" title="Vacuum flask">vacuum flask</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> He produced <a href="/wiki/Solid_hydrogen" title="Solid hydrogen">solid hydrogen</a> the next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> was discovered in December 1931 by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Urey" title="Harold Urey">Harold Urey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> was prepared in 1934 by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oliphant" title="Mark Oliphant">Mark Oliphant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Harteck" title="Paul Harteck">Paul Harteck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nostrand_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nostrand-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">Heavy water</a>, which consists of deuterium in the place of regular hydrogen, was discovered by Urey's group in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Isaac_de_Rivaz" title="François Isaac de Rivaz">François Isaac de Rivaz</a> built the first <a href="/wiki/De_Rivaz_engine" title="De Rivaz engine">de Rivaz engine</a>, an internal combustion engine powered by a mixture of hydrogen and oxygen in 1806. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Daniel_Clarke" title="Edward Daniel Clarke">Edward Daniel Clarke</a> invented the hydrogen gas blowpipe in 1819. The <a href="/wiki/D%C3%B6bereiner%27s_lamp" title="Döbereiner's lamp">Döbereiner's lamp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limelight" title="Limelight">limelight</a> were invented in 1823.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first hydrogen-filled <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloon</a> was invented by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Charles" title="Jacques Charles">Jacques Charles</a> in 1783.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> Hydrogen provided the lift for the first reliable form of air-travel following the 1852 invention of the first hydrogen-lifted airship by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Giffard" title="Henri Giffard">Henri Giffard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> German count <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Zeppelin" title="Ferdinand von Zeppelin">Ferdinand von Zeppelin</a> promoted the idea of rigid airships lifted by hydrogen that later were called <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin" title="Zeppelin">Zeppelins</a>; the first of which had its maiden flight in 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> Regularly scheduled flights started in 1910 and by the outbreak of World War I in August 1914, they had carried 35,000 passengers without a serious incident. Hydrogen-lifted airships were used as observation platforms and bombers during the war.
</p><p>The first non-stop transatlantic crossing was made by the British airship <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R34_(airship)" title="R34 (airship)">R34</a></i> in 1919. Regular passenger service resumed in the 1920s and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> reserves in the United States promised increased safety, but the U.S. government refused to sell the gas for this purpose. Therefore, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> was used in the <a href="/wiki/LZ_129_Hindenburg" title="LZ 129 Hindenburg"><i>Hindenburg</i></a> airship, which was destroyed in a midair fire over <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> on 6 May 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup> The incident was broadcast live on radio and filmed. Ignition of leaking hydrogen is widely assumed to be the cause, but later investigations pointed to the ignition of the <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminized</a> fabric coating by <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>. But the damage to hydrogen's reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> was already done and commercial hydrogen airship travel <a href="/wiki/Rigid_airship#Demise" title="Rigid airship">ceased</a>. Hydrogen is still used, in preference to non-flammable but more expensive helium, as a lifting gas for <a href="/wiki/Weather_balloon#Materials_and_equipment" title="Weather balloon">weather balloons</a>.
</p><p>In the same year, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-cooled_turbogenerator" title="Hydrogen-cooled turbogenerator">hydrogen-cooled turbogenerator</a> went into service with gaseous hydrogen as a <a href="/wiki/Coolant" title="Coolant">coolant</a> in the rotor and the stator in 1937 at <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton</a>, Ohio, by the Dayton Power &amp; Light Co.;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> because of the thermal conductivity and very low viscosity of hydrogen gas, thus lower drag than air, this is the most common type in its field today for large generators (typically 60 MW and bigger; smaller generators are usually <a href="/wiki/Air_cooling" title="Air cooling">air-cooled</a>).
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel_hydrogen_battery" title="Nickel hydrogen battery">nickel hydrogen battery</a> was used for the first time in 1977 aboard the U.S. Navy's Navigation technology satellite-2 (NTS-2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">Mars Odyssey</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> are equipped with nickel-hydrogen batteries. In the dark part of its orbit, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> is also powered by nickel-hydrogen batteries, which were finally replaced in May 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> more than 19 years after launch and 13 years beyond their design life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_quantum_theory">Role in quantum theory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emission_spectrum-H.svg"><img alt="A line spectrum showing black background with narrow lines superimposed on it: one violet, one blue, one cyan, and one red." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="757" decoding="async" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/500px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/750px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/1000px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emission_spectrum-H.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrogen emission spectrum lines in the visible range. These are the four visible lines of the <a href="/wiki/Balmer_series" title="Balmer series">Balmer series</a></div></div></div>
<p>Because of its simple atomic structure, consisting only of a proton and an electron, the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>, together with the spectrum of light produced from it or absorbed by it, has been central to the development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Furthermore, study of the corresponding simplicity of the hydrogen molecule and the corresponding cation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H2%2B" title="H2+"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>+</span><span>2</span></span></span></a> brought understanding of the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a>, which followed shortly after the quantum mechanical treatment of the hydrogen atom had been developed in the mid-1920s.
</p><p>One of the first quantum effects to be explicitly noticed (but not understood at the time) was a Maxwell observation involving hydrogen, half a century before full <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical theory</a> arrived. Maxwell observed that the <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> unaccountably departs from that of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomic" title="Diatomic">diatomic</a> gas below room temperature and begins to increasingly resemble that of a monatomic gas at cryogenic temperatures. According to quantum theory, this behavior arises from the spacing of the (quantized) rotational energy levels, which are particularly wide-spaced in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> because of its low mass. These widely spaced levels inhibit equal partition of heat energy into rotational motion in hydrogen at low temperatures. Diatomic gases composed of heavier atoms do not have such widely spaced levels and do not exhibit the same effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berman_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berman-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antihydrogen" title="Antihydrogen">Antihydrogen</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">H</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) is the <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> counterpart to hydrogen. It consists of an <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiproton</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>. Antihydrogen is the only type of antimatter atom to have been produced as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-char15_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-char15-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller15_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keller15-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_prevalence_and_distribution">Cosmic prevalence and distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg"><img alt="A white-green cotton-like clog on black background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1201" data-file-width="1127" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/220px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/330px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/440px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/NGC_604" title="NGC 604">NGC 604</a>, a giant <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">region of ionized hydrogen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">Triangulum Galaxy</a></div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen, as atomic H, is the most <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">abundant</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> in the universe, making up 75 percent of <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">normal matter</a> by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> and more than 90 percent by number of atoms. (Most of the mass of the universe, however, is not in the form of chemical-element type matter, but rather is postulated to occur as yet-undetected forms of mass such as <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>) This element is found in great abundance in stars and <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> planets. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">Molecular clouds</a> of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a>. Hydrogen plays a vital role in powering <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_reaction" title="Proton-proton reaction">proton-proton reaction</a> in case of stars with very low to approximately 1 mass of the Sun and the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> in case of stars more massive than our <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States">States</span></h3>
<p>Throughout the universe, hydrogen is mostly found in the <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> states, with properties quite distinct from those of molecular hydrogen. As a plasma, hydrogen's electron and proton are not bound together, resulting in very high electrical conductivity and high emissivity (producing the light from the Sun and other stars). The charged particles are highly influenced by magnetic and electric fields. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> they interact with the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> giving rise to <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">Birkeland currents</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(phenomenon)" title="Aurora (phenomenon)">aurora</a>.
</p><p>Hydrogen is found in the neutral atomic state in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> because the atoms seldom collide and combine. They are the source of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">21-cm hydrogen line</a> at 1420 MHz that is detected in order to probe primordial hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> The large amount of neutral hydrogen found in the <a href="/wiki/Damped_Lyman-alpha_system" title="Damped Lyman-alpha system">damped Lyman-alpha systems</a> is thought to dominate the <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> density of the universe up to a <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of <i>z</i> = 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under ordinary conditions on Earth, elemental hydrogen exists as the diatomic gas, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>. Hydrogen gas is very rare in the Earth's atmosphere (1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Part_per_million" title="Part per million">ppm</a> by volume) because of its light weight, which enables it to <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">escape from the atmosphere</a> more rapidly than heavier gases. However, hydrogen is the third most abundant element on the Earth's surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArgonneBasic_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArgonneBasic-93">[91]</a></sup> mostly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A molecular form called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protonated_molecular_hydrogen" title="Protonated molecular hydrogen">protonated molecular hydrogen</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>+</span><span>3</span></span></span>) is found in the interstellar medium, where it is generated by ionization of molecular hydrogen from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.
