This ion has also been observed in the upper atmosphere of the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The ion is relatively stable in the environment of outer space due to the low temperature and density. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>+</span><span>3</span></span></span> is one of the most abundant ions in the universe, and it plays a notable role in the chemistry of the interstellar medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> Neutral <a href="/wiki/Triatomic_hydrogen" title="Triatomic hydrogen">triatomic hydrogen</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span> can exist only in an excited form and is unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-couple_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-couple-95">[93]</a></sup> By contrast, the positive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_molecular_ion" title="Hydrogen molecular ion">hydrogen molecular ion</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>+</span><span>2</span></span></span>) is a rare molecule in the universe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Hydrogen production</a></div>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is produced in chemistry and biology laboratories, often as a by-product of other reactions; in industry for the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_and_unsaturated_compounds" title="Saturated and unsaturated compounds">unsaturated</a> substrates; and in nature as a means of expelling <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> equivalents in biochemical reactions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_electrolysis">Water electrolysis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="1416" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png/300px-Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png/450px-Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png/600px-Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_production_via_Electrolysis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrating inputs and outputs of simple electrolysis of water production of hydrogen</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a> is a simple method of producing hydrogen. A current is run through the water, and gaseous oxygen forms at the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> while gaseous hydrogen forms at the <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>. Typically the cathode is made from platinum or another inert metal when producing hydrogen for storage. If, however, the gas is to be burnt on site, oxygen is desirable to assist the combustion, and so both electrodes would be made from inert metals. (Iron, for instance, would oxidize, and thus decrease the amount of oxygen given off.) The theoretical maximum efficiency (electricity used vs. energetic value of hydrogen produced) is in the range 88–94%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O(l) → 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>(g) + O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>(g)</span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_pyrolysis">Methane pyrolysis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="1692" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png/300px-Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png/450px-Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png/600px-Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methane_Pyrolysis-1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrating inputs and outputs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_pyrolysis" title="Methane pyrolysis">methane pyrolysis</a>, a process to produce hydrogen</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen production using natural gas methane <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> is a one-step process that produces no <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> Developing volume production using this method is the key to enabling faster carbon reduction by using hydrogen in industrial processes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cell</a> electric heavy truck transportation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> and in gas turbine electric power generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> Methane pyrolysis is performed by having <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span> bubbled up through a molten metal catalyst containing dissolved <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> at 1,340 K (1,070 °C; 1,950 °F). This causes the methane to break down into hydrogen gas and solid <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, with no other byproducts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub>(g) → C(s) + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>(g)</span> (ΔH° = 74 kJ/mol)</dd></dl>
<p>The industrial quality solid carbon may be sold as manufacturing feedstock or permanently landfilled; it is not released into the atmosphere and does not cause ground water pollution in landfill. Methane pyrolysis is in development and considered suitable for commercial bulk hydrogen production. Volume production is being evaluated in the <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a> "methane pyrolysis at scale" pilot plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> Further research continues in several laboratories, including at Karlsruhe Liquid-metal Laboratory (KALLA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> and the chemical engineering laboratory at University of California – Santa Barbara<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_industrial_methods">Other industrial methods</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMR%2BWGS-1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/SMR%2BWGS-1.png/360px-SMR%2BWGS-1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/SMR%2BWGS-1.png/540px-SMR%2BWGS-1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/SMR%2BWGS-1.png/720px-SMR%2BWGS-1.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SMR%2BWGS-1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrating inputs and outputs of steam reforming of natural gas, a process to produce hydrogen</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen is often produced by reacting water with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which causes the removal of hydrogen from hydrocarbons at very high temperatures, with 48% of hydrogen production coming from <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rotech_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-rotech-115">[113]</a></sup> The water vapor is then reacted with the carbon monoxide produced by steam reforming to oxidize it to carbon dioxide and turn the water into hydrogen. Commercial bulk hydrogen is usually produced by the steam reforming of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-116">[114]</a></sup> with release of atmospheric greenhouse gas or with capture using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCS_and_climate_change_mitigation" title="CCS and climate change mitigation">CCS and climate change mitigation</a>. Steam reforming is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Bosch_reaction" title="Bosch reaction">Bosch process</a> and is widely used for the industrial preparation of hydrogen.
</p><p>At high temperatures (1000–1400 K, 700–1100 °C or 1300–2000 °F), steam (water vapor) reacts with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>.
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O → CO + 3 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>This reaction is favored at low pressures but is nonetheless conducted at high pressures (2.0 MPa, 20 atm or 600 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InHg" title="InHg">inHg</a>). This is because high-pressure <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is the most marketable product, and <a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">pressure swing adsorption</a> (PSA) purification systems work better at higher pressures. The product mixture is known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesis_gas" title="Synthesis gas">synthesis gas</a>" because it is often used directly for the production of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and related compounds. <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">Hydrocarbons</a> other than methane can be used to produce synthesis gas with varying product ratios. One of the many complications to this highly optimized technology is the formation of coke or carbon:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> → C + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Consequently, steam reforming typically employs an excess of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O</span>. Additional hydrogen can be recovered from the steam by use of carbon monoxide through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, especially with an <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> catalyst. This reaction is also a common industrial source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-116">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O → CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Other important methods for CO and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> production include partial oxidation of hydrocarbons:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">2 CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> → 2 CO + 4 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>and the coal reaction, which can serve as a prelude to the shift reaction above:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-116">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">C + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O → CO + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Hydrogen is sometimes produced and consumed in the same industrial process, without being separated. In the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">production of ammonia</a>, hydrogen is generated from natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">Electrolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> also produces hydrogen as a co-product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Olefin production units may produce substantial quantities of byproduct hydrogen particularly from cracking light feedstocks like <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal-acid">Metal-acid</span></h3>
<p>Many metals react with water to produce <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>, but the rate of hydrogen evolution depends on the metal, the pH, and the presence alloying agents. Most commonly, hydrogen evolution is induced by acids. The alkali and alkaline earth metals, aluminium, zinc, manganese, and iron react readily with aqueous acids. This reaction is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Kipp%27s_apparatus" title="Kipp's apparatus">Kipp's apparatus</a>, which once was used as a laboratory gas source:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn + 2 H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup> → Zn<sup>2+</sup> + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>In the absence of acid, the evolution of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is slower. Because iron is widely used structural material, its <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_corrosion" title="Anaerobic corrosion">anaerobic corrosion</a> is of technological significance:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O → Fe(OH)<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Many metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, are slow to react with water because they form passivated coatings of oxides. An alloy of aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, however, does react with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> At high pH, aluminium can produce <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">2 Al + 6 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O + 2 OH<sup class="template-chem2-sup">−</sup> → 2 [Al(OH)<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub>]<sup class="template-chem2-sup">−</sup> + 3 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Some metal-containing compounds react with acids to evolve <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>. Under anaerobic conditions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) can be oxidized by the protons of water to form <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>. This process is described by the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">3 Fe(OH)<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> → Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O + H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>This process occurs during the anaerobic corrosion of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">oxygen-free</a> <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and in reducing <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> below the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermochemical">Thermochemical</span></h3>
<p>More than 200 thermochemical cycles can be used for <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water splitting</a>. Many of these cycles such as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide_cycle" title="Iron oxide cycle">iron oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium(IV)_oxide%E2%80%93cerium(III)_oxide_cycle" title="Cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle">cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_zinc-oxide_cycle" title="Zinc zinc-oxide cycle">zinc zinc-oxide cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfur-iodine_cycle" title="Sulfur-iodine cycle">sulfur-iodine cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper-chlorine_cycle" title="Copper-chlorine cycle">copper-chlorine cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_sulfur_cycle" title="Hybrid sulfur cycle">hybrid sulfur cycle</a> have been evaluated for their commercial potential to produce hydrogen and oxygen from water and heat without using electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> A number of laboratories (including in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>) are developing thermochemical methods to produce hydrogen from solar energy and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serpentinization_reaction">Serpentinization reaction</span></h3>
<p>In deep geological conditions prevailing far away from the Earth's atmosphere, hydrogen (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>) is produced during the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpentinization#Hydrogen_production_by_anaerobic_oxidation_of_fayalite_ferrous_ions" title="Serpentinization">serpentinization</a>. In this process, water protons (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>) are reduced by ferrous (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<sup>2+</sup></span>) ions provided by <a href="/wiki/Fayalite" title="Fayalite">fayalite</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>SiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span>). The reaction forms <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span>), <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>), and hydrogen (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">3 Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>SiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O → 2 Fe<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + 3 SiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> + 3 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></dd>
<dd><i>fayalite + water → magnetite + quartz + hydrogen</i></dd></dl>
<p>This reaction closely resembles the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a> observed in anaerobic oxidation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> in contact with water.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petrochemical_industry">Petrochemical industry</span></h3>
<p>Large quantities of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> are used in the "upgrading" of fossil fuels. Key consumers of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> include <a href="/wiki/Hydrodealkylation" title="Hydrodealkylation">hydrodealkylation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">hydrodesulfurization</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>. Many of these reactions can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenolysis" title="Hydrogenolysis">hydrogenolysis</a>, i.e., the cleavage of bonds to carbon. Illustrative is the separation of sulfur from liquid fossil fuels:
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">R<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>S + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> → H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>S + 2 RH</span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogenation">Hydrogenation</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">Hydrogenation</a>, the addition of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> to various substrates is conducted on a large scale. The hydrogenation of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">N<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> to produce ammonia by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haber-Bosch_process" title="Haber-Bosch process">Haber-Bosch process</a> consumes a few percent of the energy budget in the entire industry. The resulting ammonia is used to supply the majority of the protein consumed by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smil_2004_Enriching_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smil_2004_Enriching-125">[123]</a></sup> Hydrogenation is used to convert <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsaturated_fat" title="Unsaturated fat">unsaturated fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">oils</a> to saturated fats and oils. The major application is the production of <a href="/wiki/Margarine" title="Margarine">margarine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">Methanol</a> is produced by hydrogenation of carbon dioxide. It is similarly the source of hydrogen in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> for the conversion of some <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a> to the metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coolant">Coolant</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen-cooled_turbo_generator" title="Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator">Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is commonly used in power stations as a coolant in generators due to a number of favorable properties that are a direct result of its light diatomic molecules. These include low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, and the highest <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of all gases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_carrier">Energy carrier</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen economy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">Hydrogen infrastructure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel" title="Hydrogen fuel">Hydrogen fuel</a></div>
<p>Elemental hydrogen has been widely discussed in the context of energy, as a possible future carrier of energy on an economy-wide scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> Hydrogen is a ''carrier'' of energy rather than an energy resource, because there is no naturally occurring source of hydrogen in useful quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustain_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen can be burned to produce heat or combined with oxygen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">fuel cells</a> to generate electricity directly, with water being the only emissions at the point of usage. The overall lifecycle emissions of hydrogen depend on how it is produced. Nearly all of the world's current supply of hydrogen is created from fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> The main method is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_methane_reforming" title="Steam methane reforming">steam methane reforming</a>, in which hydrogen is produced from a chemical reaction between steam and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, the main component of natural gas. Producing one tonne of hydrogen through this process emits 6.6–9.3 tonnes of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-131">[129]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> can remove a large fraction of these emissions, the overall carbon footprint of hydrogen from natural gas is difficult to assess as of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, in part because of emissions created in the production of the natural gas itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-:25-132">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Electricity can be used to split water molecules, producing sustainable hydrogen provided the electricity was generated sustainably. However, this <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> process is currently more expensive than creating hydrogen from methane and the efficiency of energy conversion is inherently low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-:18-133">[131]</a></sup> Hydrogen can be produced when there is a surplus of <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable electricity</a>, then stored and used to generate heat or to re-generate electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> It can be further transformed into <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Innovation in <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">hydrogen electrolysers</a> could make large-scale production of hydrogen from electricity more cost-competitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> There is potential for hydrogen to play a significant role in decarbonising energy systems because in certain sectors, replacing fossil fuels with direct use of electricity would be very difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-:18-133">[131]</a></sup> Hydrogen fuel can produce the intense heat required for industrial production of steel, cement, glass, and chemicals. For steelmaking, hydrogen can function as a clean energy carrier and simultaneously as a low-carbon catalyst replacing coal-derived <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> Hydrogen used in transportation would burn relatively cleanly, with some <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">NO<sub class="template-chem2-sub"><i>x</i></sub></span></a> emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> but without carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GATech_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-GATech-139">[137]</a></sup> Disadvantages of hydrogen as an energy carrier include high costs of storage and distribution due to hydrogen's explosivity, its large volume compared to other fuels, and its tendency to make pipes brittle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-:25-132">[130]</a></sup> The infrastructure costs associated with full conversion to a hydrogen economy would be substantial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semiconductor_industry">Semiconductor industry</span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen is employed to saturate broken ("dangling") bonds of <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a> that helps stabilizing material properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> It is also a potential <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donor</a> in various oxide materials, including <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">ZnO</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">SnO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_oxide" title="Cadmium oxide">CdO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">MgO</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">ZrO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnium(IV)_oxide" title="Hafnium(IV) oxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">HfO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthanum(III)_oxide" title="Lanthanum(III) oxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">La<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_oxide" title="Yttrium(III) oxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Y<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium_titanate" title="Strontium titanate"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">SrTiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum_aluminate" title="Lanthanum aluminate"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">LaAlO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">Al<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">ZrSiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnon" title="Hafnon"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">HfSiO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span></a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Strontium_zirconate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Strontium zirconate (page does not exist)"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">SrZrO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerospace">Aerospace</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">Liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> together serve as <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_fuel" title="Cryogenic fuel">cryogenic fuel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Liquid-propellant_rocket" title="Liquid-propellant rocket">liquid-propellant rockets</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">Space Shuttle main engines</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche_and_evolving_uses">Niche and evolving uses</span></h3>
<ul><li><b>Shielding gas:</b> Hydrogen is used as a <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> methods such as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen_welding" title="Atomic hydrogen welding">atomic hydrogen welding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Cryogenic research:</b> Liquid <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> research, including <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a> studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Buoyant lifting:</b> Because <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is lighter than air, having only 7% of the density of air, it was once widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> in balloons and <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Almqvist03_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Almqvist03-149">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Leak detection:</b> Pure or mixed with nitrogen (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Forming_gas" title="Forming gas">forming gas</a>), hydrogen is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracer_gas" title="Tracer gas">tracer gas</a> for <a href="/wiki/Leak_detection" title="Leak detection">detection</a> of minute leaks. Applications can be found in the automotive, chemical, power generation, aerospace, and telecommunications industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> Hydrogen is an authorized food additive (E 949) that allows food package leak testing, as well as having anti-oxidizing properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Neutron moderation:</b> <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> (hydrogen-2) is used in <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">nuclear fission applications</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderator</a> to slow <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>.</li>
<li><b>Nuclear fusion fuel:</b> Deuterium is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Isotopic labeling:</b> Deuterium compounds have applications in chemistry and biology in studies of <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_isotope_effect" title="Kinetic isotope effect">isotope effects</a> on reaction rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Rocket propellant:</b> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> has investigated the use of <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">rocket propellant</a> made from atomic hydrogen, boron or carbon that is frozen into solid molecular hydrogen particles that are suspended in liquid helium. Upon warming, the mixture vaporizes to allow the atomic species to recombine, heating the mixture to high temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Tritium uses:</b> <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">Tritium</a> (hydrogen-3), produced in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, is used in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bombs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> as an isotopic label in the biosciences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holte_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-holte-66">[64]</a></sup> and as a source of <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta radiation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tritium_radioluminescence" title="Tritium radioluminescence">radioluminescent paint</a> for instrument dials and emergency signage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traub95_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Traub95-61">[59]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_reactions">Biological reactions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">Biohydrogen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">Biological hydrogen production (Algae)</a></div>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is a product of some types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">anaerobic metabolism</a> and is produced by several <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, usually via reactions <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>- or <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenase" title="Hydrogenase">hydrogenases</a>. These enzymes catalyze the reversible <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction between <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> and its component two protons and two electrons. Creation of hydrogen gas occurs in the transfer of reducing equivalents produced during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> to water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> The natural cycle of hydrogen production and consumption by organisms is called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cycle" title="Hydrogen cycle">hydrogen cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhee6_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee6-156">[154]</a></sup> Hydrogen is the most abundant element in the human body in terms of numbers of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> of the element but, it is the 3rd most abundant element by mass, because hydrogen is so light. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> occurs in the breath of humans due to the metabolic activity of hydrogenase-containing microorganisms in the <a href="/wiki/Large_intestine" title="Large intestine">large intestine</a>. The concentration in fasted people at rest is typically less than 5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm) but can be 50 ppm when people with intestinal disorders consume molecules they cannot absorb during diagnostic <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_breath_test" title="Hydrogen breath test">hydrogen breath tests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup>
Hydrogen gas is produced by some bacteria and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and is a natural component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatus" title="Flatus">flatus</a>, as is <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, itself a hydrogen source of increasing importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">Water splitting</a>, in which water is decomposed into its component protons, electrons, and oxygen, occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reactions" title="Light-dependent reactions">light reactions</a> in all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> organisms. Some such organisms, including the alga <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas_reinhardtii" title="Chlamydomonas reinhardtii">Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, have evolved a second step in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_reaction" title="Dark reaction">dark reactions</a> in which protons and electrons are reduced to form <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> gas by specialized hydrogenases in the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> Efforts have been undertaken to genetically modify cyanobacterial hydrogenases to efficiently synthesize <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> gas even in the presence of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup> Efforts have also been undertaken with genetically modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">alga in a bioreactor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_precautions">Safety and precautions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_safety" title="Hydrogen safety">Hydrogen safety</a></div>

</td></tr>
</tbody></table><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Chemical compound</div>
<p>Hydrogen poses a number of hazards to human safety, from potential <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonations</a> and fires when mixed with air to being an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiant</a> in its pure, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-free form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAH2_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-163">[161]</a></sup> In addition, liquid hydrogen is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogen" title="Cryogen">cryogen</a> and presents dangers (such as <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">frostbite</a>) associated with very cold liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> Hydrogen dissolves in many metals and in addition to leaking out, may have adverse effects on them, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> leading to cracks and explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> Hydrogen gas leaking into external air may spontaneously ignite. Moreover, hydrogen fire, while being extremely hot, is almost invisible, and thus can lead to accidental burns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunn88_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunn88-167">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even interpreting the hydrogen data (including safety data) is confounded by a number of phenomena. Many physical and chemical properties of hydrogen depend on the <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">parahydrogen/orthohydrogen</a> ratio (it often takes days or weeks at a given temperature to reach the equilibrium ratio, for which the data is usually given). Hydrogen detonation parameters, such as critical detonation pressure and temperature, strongly depend on the container geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAH2_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-163">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
